Title: History of artificial intelligence - Wikipedia
[Jump to content](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#bodyContent)

[![Page semi-protected](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/en/thumb/1/1b/Semi-protection-shackle.svg/20px-Semi-protection-shackle.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wikipedia:Protection_policy#semi "This article is semi-protected until May 26, 2026 at 03:17 UTC.")

From Wikipedia, the free encyclopedia

See also: [Timeline of artificial intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Timeline_of_artificial_intelligence "Timeline of artificial intelligence") and [Progress in artificial intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Progress_in_artificial_intelligence "Progress in artificial intelligence")

The **history of artificial intelligence** ( **AI**) began in [antiquity](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_history "Ancient history"), with myths, stories, and rumors of artificial beings endowed with intelligence or consciousness by master craftsmen. The study of logic and formal reasoning from antiquity to the present led to the development of the [programmable digital computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer "Computer") in the 1940s, a machine predicated on abstract [mathematical reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_reasoning "Mathematical reasoning"). This device and the ideas behind it inspired scientists to begin discussing the possibility of building an [electronic brain](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Electronic_brain "Electronic brain").

The field of AI research was founded at a [workshop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dartmouth_workshop "Dartmouth workshop") held on the campus of [Dartmouth College](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dartmouth_College "Dartmouth College") in 1956.[\[1\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEKaplanHaenlein2018-1) At the workshop, the first AI program, [Logic Theorist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic_Theorist "Logic Theorist"), was presented by future Turing Awardee [Allen Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell") and future Nobel Laureate [Herbert A. Simon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon "Herbert A. Simon"), with help from [J. C. Shaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cliff_Shaw "Cliff Shaw"). Attendees of the workshop became the leaders of AI research for decades. Many of them predicted that machines as intelligent as humans would exist within a generation. The [U.S. government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA") provided millions of dollars with the hope of making this vision come true.[\[2\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist1994143%E2%80%93156-2)

It became apparent that researchers had underestimated the complexity of this objective.[\[3\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist1994144%E2%80%93152-3) In 1974, criticism from [James Lighthill](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/James_Lighthill "James Lighthill") and pressure from the U.S. Congress led the U.S. and [British Governments](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_Governments "British Governments") to stop funding undirected research into artificial intelligence. Seven years later, a visionary initiative by the [Japanese Government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Japanese_Government "Japanese Government") and the success of [expert systems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Expert_system "Expert system") reinvigorated investment in AI, and by the late 1980s, the industry had grown into a billion-dollar enterprise. However, investors' enthusiasm waned in the 1990s, and the field was criticized in the press and avoided by industry (a period known as an " [AI winter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_winter "AI winter")"). Nevertheless, research and funding continued to grow under other names.

In the early 2000s, [machine learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machine_learning "Machine learning") was applied to a wide range of problems in academia and industry. The success was attributable to the availability of powerful computer hardware, the accumulation of expansive data sets, and the application of rigorous mathematical methods. Soon after, [deep learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Deep_learning "Deep learning") proved to be a breakthrough technology, eclipsing all other methods. The [transformer architecture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Transformer_(deep_learning_architecture) "Transformer (deep learning architecture)"), introduced in 2017, and was utilized to produce [generative AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Generative_AI "Generative AI") applications, among other implementation.

Investment in AI [boomed](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_boom "AI boom") in the 2020s. The recent AI boom, initiated by the development of transformer architecture, led to the rapid scaling and public releases of [large language models](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Large_language_models "Large language models") (LLMs) like [ChatGPT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ChatGPT "ChatGPT"). These models exhibit human-like traits of knowledge, attention, and creativity, and have been integrated into various sectors, fueling exponential investment in AI. However, concerns about the potential risks and [ethical implications of advanced AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence "Ethics of artificial intelligence") have also emerged, causing debate about the future of AI and its impact on society.

## Precursors

### Myth, folklore, fiction and speculation

Main article: [Artificial intelligence in fiction](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence_in_fiction "Artificial intelligence in fiction")

Mechanical men and artificial beings appear in [Greek myths](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Greek_myth "Greek myth"), such as the golden robots of [Hephaestus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hephaestus "Hephaestus"),[\[4\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck5-4) the bronze giant [Talos](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Talos "Talos")[\[5\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERhodios2007Book_4,_the_Talos_episode-5) and [Pygmalion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pygmalion_(mythology) "Pygmalion (mythology)")'s [Galatea](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Galatea_(mythological_statue) "Galatea (mythological statue)")[\[6\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMorford2007-6). In the Middle Ages, there were rumors of secret mystical or [alchemical](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alchemy "Alchemy") means of placing mind into matter, such as [Jabir ibn Hayyan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jabir_ibn_Hayyan "Jabir ibn Hayyan")'s _[Takwin](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Takwin "Takwin")_,[\[7\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEO'Connor1994-7) [Paracelsus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paracelsus "Paracelsus")' [homunculus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Homunculus "Homunculus"),[\[8\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTELinden2003-8) [Rabbi Judah Loew](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Judah_Loew "Judah Loew")'s [Golem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Golem "Golem")[\[9\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEJewish_EncyclopediaGOLEM-9) and [Roger Bacon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Bacon "Roger Bacon")'s [brazen head](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Brazen_head "Brazen head").[\[10\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist199438-10) By the 19th century, ideas about artificial men and thinking machines became a popular theme in fiction. Notable works include [Mary Shelley](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mary_Shelley "Mary Shelley")'s _[Frankenstein](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frankenstein "Frankenstein")_ (1818), [Johann Wolfgang von Goethe](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johann_Wolfgang_von_Goethe "Johann Wolfgang von Goethe")'s, _[Faust, Part Two](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Faust,_Part_Two "Faust, Part Two")_ (1832), and [Karel Čapek](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karel_%C4%8Capek "Karel Čapek")'s _[R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/R.U.R._(Rossum%27s_Universal_Robots) "R.U.R. (Rossum's Universal Robots)")_ (1921).[\[11\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEGoethe1890-11)[\[12\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck200417%E2%80%9325-12) Stories of these creatures and their fates consider many of the same [hopes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Superintelligence "Superintelligence"), [fears](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Existential_risk_from_artificial_intelligence "Existential risk from artificial intelligence") and [ethical concerns](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ethics_of_artificial_intelligence "Ethics of artificial intelligence") that are presented by modern artificial intelligence.[\[13\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004xviii,_3,_340%E2%80%93400-13) Issues relevant to AI were also discussed in speculative essays such as [Samuel Butler](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Samuel_Butler_(novelist) "Samuel Butler (novelist)")'s " [Darwin among the Machines](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Darwin_among_the_Machines "Darwin among the Machines")" (1863),[\[14\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEButler1863-14)

### Automata

Main article: [Automaton](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automaton "Automaton")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/2/2f/Al-jazari_robots.jpg/250px-Al-jazari_robots.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Al-jazari_robots.jpg) [Al-Jazari](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Jazari "Al-Jazari")'s programmable automata (1206 CE)

Realistic humanoid [automata](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automata "Automata") were built by craftsman from many civilizations, including [Yan Shi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Yan_Shi "Yan Shi"),[\[15\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENeedham198653-15) [Hero of Alexandria](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hero_of_Alexandria "Hero of Alexandria"),[\[16\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck20046-16) [Al-Jazari](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Jazari "Al-Jazari"),[\[17\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENick2005-17) [Haroun al-Rashid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Haroun_al-Rashid "Haroun al-Rashid"),[\[18\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck200410-18) [Jacques de Vaucanson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Jacques_de_Vaucanson "Jacques de Vaucanson"),[\[19\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist199440-19)[\[20\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck200416-20) [Leonardo Torres y Quevedo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonardo_Torres_y_Quevedo "Leonardo Torres y Quevedo"),[\[21\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck200459%E2%80%9362-21) [Pierre Jaquet-Droz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Pierre_Jaquet-Droz "Pierre Jaquet-Droz") and [Wolfgang von Kempelen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Wolfgang_von_Kempelen "Wolfgang von Kempelen").[\[22\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck200417-22)[\[23\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTELevitt2000-23) The oldest known automata were sacred statues of [ancient Egypt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Egypt "Ancient Egypt") and [Greece](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ancient_Greece "Ancient Greece").[\[24\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist199430-24)[\[25\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier19931-25) The faithful believed that craftsman had imbued these figures with very real minds, capable of wisdom and emotion— [Hermes Trismegistus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hermes_Trismegistus "Hermes Trismegistus") wrote that "by discovering the true nature of the gods, man has been able to reproduce it".[\[26\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-26)

### Formal reasoning

See also: [Reason § Philosophical history](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Reason#Philosophical_history "Reason")

Artificial intelligence is based on the assumption that the process of human thought can be mechanized. Chinese, Indian and Greek philosophers developed structured methods of formal reasoning by the first millennium BCE. [Formal logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic "Logic") was invented and improved by Greek, Islamic and European philosophers, such as [Aristotle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Aristotle "Aristotle"), [Euclid](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Euclid "Euclid"), [Al-Khwarizmi](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Al-Khwarizmi "Al-Khwarizmi"), [Duns Scotus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Duns_Scotus "Duns Scotus") and [René Descartes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes "René Descartes").

Spanish philosopher [Ramon Llull](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ramon_Llull "Ramon Llull") (1232–1315) developed several _logical machines_ devoted to the production of knowledge by logical means;[\[27\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECarreras_y_Artau2018-27)[\[28\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20216-28) Llull described his machines as mechanical entities that could combine basic and undeniable truths by simple logical operations, produced by the machine by mechanical meanings, in such ways as to produce all the possible knowledge.[\[29\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonner2007-29) Llull's work had a great influence on [Gottfried Leibniz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz "Gottfried Leibniz"), who redeveloped his ideas.[\[30\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBonner198557%E2%80%9371-30)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz%2C_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg/250px-Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz%2C_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Gottfried_Wilhelm_Leibniz,_Bernhard_Christoph_Francke.jpg) [Gottfried Leibniz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz "Gottfried Leibniz"), who speculated that human reason could be reduced to mechanical calculation

In the 17th century, [Leibniz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz "Gottfried Leibniz"), [Thomas Hobbes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Thomas_Hobbes "Thomas Hobbes") and [René Descartes](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ren%C3%A9_Descartes "René Descartes") explored the possibility that all rational thought could be made as systematic as algebra or geometry.[\[31\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-31) Hobbes wrote in _[Leviathan](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leviathan_(Hobbes_book) "Leviathan (Hobbes book)")_: "For _reason_ ... is nothing but _reckoning_, that is adding and subtracting".[\[32\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-32) Leibniz described a universal language of reasoning, the _[characteristica universalis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Characteristica_universalis "Characteristica universalis")_, which would reduce argumentation to calculation so that "there would be no more need of disputation between two philosophers than between two accountants. For it would suffice to take their pencils in hand, down to their slates, and to say to each other (with a friend as witness, if they liked): _Let us calculate_."[\[33\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-33)

[Boole](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Boole "George Boole")'s _[The Laws of Thought](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Laws_of_Thought "The Laws of Thought")_ (1854) and [Frege](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frege "Frege")'s _[Begriffsschrift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Begriffsschrift "Begriffsschrift")_ (1879) defined the modern form of symbolic [mathematical logic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mathematical_logic "Mathematical logic").[\[34\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20218-34) Building on Frege's system, [Russell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bertrand_Russell "Bertrand Russell") and [Whitehead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead "Alfred North Whitehead") presented a formal treatment of the foundations of arithmetic in the _[Principia Mathematica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principia_Mathematica "Principia Mathematica")_ in 1913. Inspired by their success, [David Hilbert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_program "Hilbert's program") challenged mathematicians of the 1920s and 30s to [formalize all mathematical reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hilbert%27s_Program "Hilbert's Program"). In response, [Gödel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kurt_G%C3%B6del "Kurt Gödel")'s [incompleteness proof](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorems "Gödel's incompleteness theorems"),[\[35\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20219-35) [Turing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alan_Turing "Alan Turing")'s [machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turing_machine "Turing machine")[\[35\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20219-35) and [Church](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alonzo_Church "Alonzo Church")'s [Lambda calculus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lambda_calculus "Lambda calculus")[\[a\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-37) showed that there were, in fact, limits to what formal mathematics could do.

However, within these limits, the [Church-Turing thesis](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Church-Turing_thesis "Church-Turing thesis") implied that a mechanical device, shuffling symbols as simple as _0_ and _1_, could imitate any conceivable process of mathematical reasoning or problem solving.[\[35\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20219-35) The key insight was the [Turing machine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turing_machine "Turing machine")—a simple theoretical construct that captured the essence of abstract symbol manipulation.[\[37\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-38) This device and the ideas behind it would inspire engineers and mathematicians of the 1940s to build machines that could theoretically carry out any form of formal reasoning or problem solving.

### Computer science

Main articles: [History of computer hardware](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_computer_hardware "History of computer hardware") and [History of computer science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_computer_science "History of computer science")

Calculating machines were designed or built in antiquity and throughout history by many people, including [Gottfried Leibniz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gottfried_Leibniz#Information_technology "Gottfried Leibniz"),[\[28\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig20216-28)[\[38\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECouturat1901-39) [Joseph Marie Jacquard](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Marie_Jacquard "Joseph Marie Jacquard"),[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202115-40) [Charles Babbage](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Babbage "Charles Babbage"),[\[39\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202115-40)[\[40\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist199467-41) [Percy Ludgate](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Percy_Ludgate "Percy Ludgate"),[\[41\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-42) [Leonardo Torres Quevedo](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Leonardo_Torres_Quevedo "Leonardo Torres Quevedo"),[\[42\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-43) [Vannevar Bush](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Vannevar_Bush "Vannevar Bush"),[\[43\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERandall198213,_16%E2%80%9317-44) and others. [Ada Lovelace](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ada_Lovelace "Ada Lovelace") speculated that Babbage's machine was "a thinking or ... reasoning machine", but warned "It is desirable to guard against the possibility of exaggerated ideas that arise as to the powers" of the machine.[\[44\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-45)[\[45\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMenabreaLovelace1843-46)

The first modern computers were the massive machines of the [Second World War](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Second_World_War "Second World War") (such as [Konrad Zuse](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Konrad_Zuse "Konrad Zuse")'s [Z3](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Z3_(computer) "Z3 (computer)"), [Tommy Flowers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Tommy_Flowers "Tommy Flowers")' [Heath Robinson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heath_Robinson_machine "Heath Robinson machine") and [Colossus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Colossus_computer "Colossus computer"), [Atanasoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Vincent_Atanasoff "John Vincent Atanasoff") and [Berry](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Clifford_Berry "Clifford Berry")'s [ABC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Atanasoff%E2%80%93Berry_computer "Atanasoff–Berry computer"), and [ENIAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ENIAC "ENIAC") at the [University of Pennsylvania](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Pennsylvania "University of Pennsylvania")).[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202114-47) [ENIAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ENIAC "ENIAC") was based on the theoretical foundation laid by [Alan Turing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alan_Turing "Alan Turing") and developed by [John von Neumann](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_von_Neumann "John von Neumann"),[\[47\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck200476%E2%80%9380-48) and proved to be the most influential.[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202114-47)

## Birth of artificial intelligence (1941–1956)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/10/BRL61-IBM_702.jpg/500px-BRL61-IBM_702.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:BRL61-IBM_702.jpg) The [IBM 702](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM_702 "IBM 702"): a computer used by the first generation of AI researchers.

The earliest research into thinking machines was inspired by a confluence of ideas that became prevalent in the late 1930s, 1940s, and early 1950s. Recent research in [neurology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neurology "Neurology") had shown that the brain was an electrical network of [neurons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Neuron "Neuron") that fired in all-or-nothing pulses. [Norbert Wiener](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Norbert_Wiener "Norbert Wiener")'s [cybernetics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cybernetic "Cybernetic") described control and stability in electrical networks. [Claude Shannon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claude_Shannon "Claude Shannon")'s [information theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Information_theory "Information theory") described digital signals (i.e., all-or-nothing signals). [Alan Turing](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alan_Turing "Alan Turing")'s [theory of computation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Theory_of_computation "Theory of computation") showed that any form of computation could be described digitally. The close relationship between these ideas suggested that it might be possible to construct an "electronic brain".

In the 1940s and 50s, a handful of scientists from a variety of fields (mathematics, psychology, engineering, economics and political science) explored several research directions that would be vital to later AI research.[\[48\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-49) Alan Turing was among the first people to seriously investigate the theoretical possibility of "machine intelligence".[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECopeland2004-50) The field of " [artificial intelligence research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_intelligence "Artificial intelligence")" was founded as an academic discipline in 1956.[\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-Dartmouth_workshop-51)

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/5/55/Turing_test_diagram.png/250px-Turing_test_diagram.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Turing_test_diagram.png) Turing test[\[51\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-52)

### Turing test

Main article: [Turing test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turing_test "Turing test")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/c/ce/Alan_turing_header.jpg/250px-Alan_turing_header.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Alan_turing_header.jpg) Alan Turing in 1951

In 1950, Turing published a landmark paper, " [Computing Machinery and Intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computing_Machinery_and_Intelligence "Computing Machinery and Intelligence")", in which he speculated about the possibility of creating machines that think.[\[52\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-53)[\[b\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-54) In the paper, he noted that "thinking" is difficult to define and devised his famous [Turing test](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turing_Test "Turing Test"): If a machine could carry on a conversation (over a [teleprinter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Teleprinter "Teleprinter")) that was indistinguishable from a conversation with a human being, then it was reasonable to say that the machine was "thinking".[\[53\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist199492%E2%80%9398-55) This simplified version of the problem allowed Turing to argue convincingly that a "thinking machine" was at least _plausible_ and the paper answered all the most common objections to the proposition.[\[54\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig2021981-56) The Turing test was the first serious proposal in the [philosophy of artificial intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy_of_artificial_intelligence "Philosophy of artificial intelligence").

### Artificial neural networks

[Walter Pitts](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Walter_Pitts "Walter Pitts") and [Warren McCulloch](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Warren_Sturgis_McCulloch "Warren Sturgis McCulloch") analyzed networks of idealized [artificial neurons](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_neuron "Artificial neuron") and showed how they might perform simple logical functions in 1943. They were the first to describe what later researchers would call a [neural network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_neural_network "Artificial neural network").[\[55\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-57) The paper was influenced by Turing's paper " [On Computable Numbers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turing%27s_proof "Turing's proof")" from 1936, using similar two-state boolean 'neurons', but was the first to apply it to neuronal function.[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECopeland2004-50) One of the students inspired by Pitts and McCulloch was [Marvin Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky") who was a 24-year-old graduate student at the time. In 1951, Minsky and Dean Edmonds built the first neural net machine, the [SNARC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stochastic_Neural_Analog_Reinforcement_Calculator "Stochastic Neural Analog Reinforcement Calculator").[\[56\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-58) Minsky would later become one of the most important leaders and innovators in Artificial Intelligence.

### Cybernetic robots

Experimental robots such as [William Grey Walter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/William_Grey_Walter "William Grey Walter")'s [turtles](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Turtle_(robot) "Turtle (robot)") and the [Johns Hopkins Beast](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Johns_Hopkins_Beast "Johns Hopkins Beast"), were built in the 1950s. These machines did not use computers, digital electronics, or symbolic reasoning; they were controlled entirely by analog circuitry.[\[57\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-59)

### Game AI

In 1951, using the [Ferranti Mark 1](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ferranti_Mark_1 "Ferranti Mark 1") machine of the [University of Manchester](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/University_of_Manchester "University of Manchester"), [Christopher Strachey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Christopher_Strachey "Christopher Strachey") wrote a checkers program[\[58\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202117-60) and [Dietrich Prinz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dietrich_Prinz "Dietrich Prinz") wrote one for chess.[\[59\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECopeland1999-61) [Arthur Samuel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_Samuel_(computer_scientist) "Arthur Samuel (computer scientist)")'s checkers program, the subject of his 1959 paper "Some Studies in Machine Learning Using the Game of Checkers", eventually achieved sufficient skill to challenge a respectable amateur.[\[60\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchaeffer1997Chapter_6-62) Samuel's program was among the first uses of what would later be called [machine learning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machine_learning "Machine learning").[\[61\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202117,_19-63) [Game AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Game_AI "Game AI") would continue to be used as a measure of progress in AI throughout its history.

### Symbolic reasoning and the Logic Theorist

Main article: [Logic Theorist](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic_Theorist "Logic Theorist")

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/f/f7/Herbert_A._Simon_and_Allen_Newell_Chess_Match.jpg/250px-Herbert_A._Simon_and_Allen_Newell_Chess_Match.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Herbert_A._Simon_and_Allen_Newell_Chess_Match.jpg) Herbert Simon (left) in a chess match against Allen Newell c. 1958

When access to [digital computers](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Digital_computer "Digital computer") became possible in the mid-fifties, a few scientists instinctively recognized that a machine that could manipulate numbers could also manipulate symbols and that the manipulation of symbols could well be the essence of human thought. This was a new approach to creating thinking machines.[\[62\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004137%E2%80%93170-64)[\[63\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199344%E2%80%9347-65)

In 1955, [Allen Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell") and future Nobel Laureate [Herbert A. Simon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon "Herbert A. Simon") created the "Logic Theorist", with help from [J. C. Shaw](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cliff_Shaw "Cliff Shaw"). The program would eventually prove 38 of the first 52 theorems in [Russell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bertrand_Russell "Bertrand Russell") and [Whitehead](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Alfred_North_Whitehead "Alfred North Whitehead")'s _[Principia Mathematica](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Principia_Mathematica "Principia Mathematica")_, and find new and more elegant proofs for some.[\[64\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-66) Simon said that they had "solved the venerable [mind/body problem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mind/body_problem "Mind/body problem"), explaining how a system composed of matter can have the properties of mind."[\[65\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-67)[\[c\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-68) The symbolic reasoning paradigm they introduced would dominate AI research and funding until the mid-90s, as well as inspire the [cognitive revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_revolution "Cognitive revolution").

### Dartmouth Workshop

Main article: [Dartmouth workshop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dartmouth_workshop "Dartmouth workshop")

The Dartmouth workshop of 1956 was a pivotal event that marked the formal inception of AI as an academic discipline.[\[50\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-Dartmouth_workshop-51) It was organized by [Marvin Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky") and [John McCarthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)"), with the support of two senior scientists [Claude Shannon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Claude_Shannon "Claude Shannon") and [Nathan Rochester](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nathaniel_Rochester_(computer_scientist) "Nathaniel Rochester (computer scientist)") of [IBM](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/IBM "IBM"). The proposal for the conference stated they intended to test the assertion that "every aspect of learning or any other feature of intelligence can be so precisely described that a machine can be made to simulate it".[\[66\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCarthyMinskyRochesterShannon1955-69)[\[d\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-72) The term "Artificial Intelligence" was introduced by John McCarthy at the workshop.[\[e\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-76)
The participants included [Ray Solomonoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ray_Solomonoff "Ray Solomonoff"), [Oliver Selfridge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Oliver_Selfridge "Oliver Selfridge"), [Trenchard More](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Trenchard_More "Trenchard More"), [Arthur Samuel](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Arthur_Samuel_(computer_scientist) "Arthur Samuel (computer scientist)"), [Allen Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell") and [Herbert A. Simon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon "Herbert A. Simon"), all of whom would create important programs during the first decades of AI research.[\[72\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004129%E2%80%93130-77)[\[f\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-78) At the workshop, Newell and Simon debuted the "Logic Theorist".[\[73\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004125-79) The workshop was the moment that AI gained its name, its mission, its first major success and its key players, and is widely considered the birth of AI.[\[g\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-81)

### Cognitive revolution

Main article: [cognitive revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_revolution "Cognitive revolution")

In the autumn of 1956, Newell and Simon also presented the Logic Theorist at a meeting of the Special Interest Group in Information Theory at the [Massachusetts Institute of Technology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Massachusetts_Institute_of_Technology "Massachusetts Institute of Technology") (MIT). At the same meeting, [Noam Chomsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Noam_Chomsky "Noam Chomsky") discussed his [generative grammar](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Generative_grammar "Generative grammar"), and [George Miller](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/George_Armitage_Miller "George Armitage Miller") described his landmark paper " [The Magical Number Seven, Plus or Minus Two](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/The_Magical_Number_Seven,_Plus_or_Minus_Two "The Magical Number Seven, Plus or Minus Two")".[\[46\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202114-47) Miller wrote "I left the symposium with a conviction, more intuitive than rational, that experimental psychology, theoretical linguistics, and the computer simulation of cognitive processes were all pieces from a larger whole."[\[75\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMiller2003-82)

This meeting was the beginning of the " [cognitive revolution](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_revolution "Cognitive revolution")"—an interdisciplinary [paradigm shift](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Paradigm_shift "Paradigm shift") in psychology, philosophy, computer science and neuroscience. It inspired the creation of the sub-fields of [symbolic artificial intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolic_artificial_intelligence "Symbolic artificial intelligence"), [generative linguistics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Generative_linguistics "Generative linguistics"), [cognitive science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_science "Cognitive science"), [cognitive psychology](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_psychology "Cognitive psychology"), [cognitive neuroscience](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cognitive_neuroscience "Cognitive neuroscience") and the philosophical schools of [computationalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computationalism "Computationalism") and [functionalism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Functionalism_(philosophy) "Functionalism (philosophy)"). All these fields used related tools to model the mind and results discovered in one field were relevant to the others.

The cognitive approach allowed researchers to consider "mental objects" like thoughts, plans, goals, facts or memories, often analyzed using [high level symbols](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Physical_symbol_system "Physical symbol system") in functional networks. These objects had been forbidden as "unobservable" by earlier paradigms such as [behaviorism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Behaviorism "Behaviorism").[\[h\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-84) [Symbolic mental objects](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolic_AI "Symbolic AI") would become the major focus of AI research and funding for the next several decades.

## Early successes (1956–1974)

The programs developed in the years after the [Dartmouth Workshop](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Dartmouth_workshop "Dartmouth workshop") were, to most people, simply "astonishing":[\[i\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-87) computers were solving algebra word problems, proving theorems in geometry and learning to speak English. Few at the time would have believed that such "intelligent" behavior by machines was possible at all.[\[79\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199352%E2%80%93107-88)[\[80\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoravec19889-89)[\[78\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202118-86) Researchers expressed an intense optimism in private and in print, predicting that a fully intelligent machine would be built in less than 20 years.[\[81\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-90) Government agencies like the [Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Defense_Advanced_Research_Projects_Agency "Defense Advanced Research Projects Agency") (DARPA, then known as "ARPA") poured money into the field.[\[82\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-91) Artificial Intelligence laboratories were set up at many British and US universities in the latter 1950s and early 1960s.[\[49\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECopeland2004-50)

[Stanisław Lem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanis%C5%82aw_Lem "Stanisław Lem")'s philosophical essay on "intellectronics" appeared in Lem's _[Summa Technologiae](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Summa_Technologiae "Summa Technologiae")_ in 1964.[\[83\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-92)

### Approaches

There were many successful programs and new directions in the late 50s and 1960s. Some of the most influential included:

#### Reasoning, planning and problem solving as search

Many early AI programs used the same basic [algorithm](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Algorithm "Algorithm"). To achieve some goal (like winning a game or proving a theorem), they proceeded step by step towards it (by making a move or a deduction) as if searching through a maze, [backtracking](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Backtracking "Backtracking") whenever they reached a dead end.[\[84\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-93) The principal difficulty was that, for many problems, the number of possible paths through the "maze" was astronomical (a situation known as a " [combinatorial explosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Combinatorial_explosion "Combinatorial explosion")"). Researchers would reduce the search space by using [heuristics](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Heuristics "Heuristics") that would eliminate paths that were unlikely to lead to a solution.[\[85\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004246-94)

[Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell") and [Simon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon "Herbert A. Simon") tried to capture a general version of this algorithm in a program called the " [General Problem Solver](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/General_Problem_Solver "General Problem Solver")".[\[86\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004245%E2%80%93250-95)[\[87\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202119,_106-96) Other "searching" programs accomplished impressive results like solving problems in geometry and algebra, such as [Herbert Gelernter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_Gelernter "Herbert Gelernter")'s Geometry Theorem Prover (1958)[\[88\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202119-97) and Symbolic Automatic Integrator (SAINT), written by [Minsky's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky") student James Slagle in 1961.[\[89\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199351%E2%80%9358,_65%E2%80%9366-98)[\[90\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202120-99) Other programs searched through goals and subgoals to [plan actions](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automated_planning_and_scheduling "Automated planning and scheduling"), like the [STRIPS](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_Research_Institute_Problem_Solver "Stanford Research Institute Problem Solver") system developed at [Stanford](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford "Stanford") to control the behavior of the robot [Shakey](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Shakey_the_Robot "Shakey the Robot").[\[91\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-100)

#### Natural language

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/6/67/Semantic_Net.svg/250px-Semantic_Net.svg.png)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:Semantic_Net.svg) An example of a [semantic network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semantic_network "Semantic network")

An important goal of AI research is to allow computers to communicate in [natural languages](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_language_processing "Natural language processing") like English. An early success was [Daniel Bobrow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Daniel_Bobrow "Daniel Bobrow")'s program [STUDENT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/STUDENT_(computer_program) "STUDENT (computer program)"), which could solve high-school algebra word-problems.[\[92\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-101)

A [semantic net](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Semantic_net "Semantic net") represents concepts (e.g., "house", "door") as nodes, and relations among concepts as links between the nodes (e.g. "has-a"). Ross Quillian wrote the first AI program to use a semantic net,[\[93\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199379%E2%80%9383-102) and the most successful (and controversial) version was [Roger Schank](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Roger_Schank "Roger Schank")'s [Conceptual dependency theory](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Conceptual_dependency_theory "Conceptual dependency theory").[\[94\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993164%E2%80%93172-103)

[Joseph Weizenbaum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Weizenbaum "Joseph Weizenbaum")'s [ELIZA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ELIZA "ELIZA") could carry out conversations that were so realistic that users occasionally were fooled into thinking they were communicating with a human being and not with a computer program (see [ELIZA effect](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ELIZA_effect "ELIZA effect")). But in fact, ELIZA simply gave a [canned response](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Canned_response "Canned response") or repeated back what was said to it, rephrasing its response with a few grammar-rules. ELIZA was the first [chatbot](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chatbot "Chatbot").[\[95\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004291%E2%80%93296-104)[\[96\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993134%E2%80%93139-105)

#### Micro-worlds

In the late 60s, [Marvin Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky") and [Seymour Papert](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seymour_Papert "Seymour Papert") of the [MIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIT "MIT") AI Laboratory proposed that AI research should focus on artificially simple situations known as micro-worlds.[\[j\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-106) They pointed out that in successful sciences like physics, basic principles were often best understood using simplified models like frictionless planes or perfectly rigid bodies. Much of the research focused on a " [blocks world](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Blocks_world "Blocks world")", which consists of colored blocks of various shapes and sizes arrayed on a flat surface.[\[97\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-Blocks_world-107)

This paradigm led to innovative work in [machine vision](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Machine_vision "Machine vision") by [Gerald Sussman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerald_Sussman "Gerald Sussman"), Adolfo Guzman, [David Waltz](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/David_Waltz "David Waltz") (who invented " [constraint propagation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Constraint_propagation "Constraint propagation")"), and especially [Patrick Winston](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Patrick_Winston "Patrick Winston"). At the same time, Minsky and Papert built a robot arm that could stack blocks, bringing the blocks world to life. [Terry Winograd](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Terry_Winograd "Terry Winograd")'s [SHRDLU](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SHRDLU "SHRDLU") could communicate in ordinary English sentences about the micro-world, plan operations and execute them.[\[97\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-Blocks_world-107)

#### Perceptrons and early neural networks

Main article: [Perceptron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perceptron "Perceptron")

In the 1960s, research-funders primarily supported laboratories researching [symbolic AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolic_artificial_intelligence "Symbolic artificial intelligence"), however, a few laboratories pursued research in neural networks.

[![](https://upload.wikimedia.org/wikipedia/commons/thumb/1/1a/330-PSA-80-60_%28USN_710739%29_%2820897323365%29.jpg/250px-330-PSA-80-60_%28USN_710739%29_%2820897323365%29.jpg)](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/File:330-PSA-80-60_(USN_710739)_(20897323365).jpg) [The Mark 1 Perceptron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mark_I_Perceptron "Mark I Perceptron")

The [perceptron](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perceptron "Perceptron"), a single-layer [neural network](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Artificial_neural_network "Artificial neural network") was introduced in 1958 by [Frank Rosenblatt](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Frank_Rosenblatt "Frank Rosenblatt")[\[98\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-108) (who had been a schoolmate of [Marvin Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky") at the [Bronx High School of Science](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bronx_High_School_of_Science "Bronx High School of Science")).[\[99\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993102-109) Like most AI researchers, he was optimistic about their power, predicting that a perceptron "may eventually be able to learn, make decisions, and translate languages".[\[100\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-110) Rosenblatt was primarily funded by the [Office of Naval Research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Office_of_Naval_Research "Office of Naval Research").[\[101\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERosenblatt1962-111)

[Bernard Widrow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernard_Widrow "Bernard Widrow") and his student [Ted Hoff](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marcian_Hoff "Marcian Hoff") built [ADALINE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ADALINE "ADALINE") (1960) and [MADALINE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ADALINE "ADALINE") (1962), which had up to 1000 adjustable weights.[\[102\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202120%E2%80%9321-112)[\[103\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWidrowLehr1990-113) A group at [Stanford Research Institute](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SRI_International "SRI International") led by [Charles A. Rosen](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Charles_Rosen_(scientist) "Charles Rosen (scientist)") and Alfred E. (Ted) Brain built two neural-network machines named MINOS I (1960) and II (1963), mainly funded by the [U.S. Army Signal Corps](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_Army_Signal_Corps "United States Army Signal Corps"). MINOS II[\[104\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERosenNilssonAdams1965-114) had 6600 adjustable weights,[\[105\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENilsson1984-115) and was controlled with an [SDS 910 computer](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SDS_9_Series "SDS 9 Series") in a configuration named MINOS III (1968), which could classify symbols on army maps, and recognize hand-printed characters on [Fortran](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Fortran "Fortran") [coding sheets](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_programming_in_the_punched_card_era "Computer programming in the punched card era").[\[106\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHartNilssonPerraultMitchell2003-116)[\[107\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENielson2005-117) Most of neural-network research during this early period involved building and using bespoke hardware, rather than simulation on digital computers.[\[k\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-119)

However, partly due to lack of results and partly due to competition from [symbolic AI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Symbolic_AI "Symbolic AI") research, the MINOS project ran out of funding in 1966. Rosenblatt failed to secure continued funding in the 1960s.[\[108\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlazaran_Rodriguez1991-118) In 1969, research came to a sudden halt with the publication of [Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky") and [Papert's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Seymour_Papert "Seymour Papert") 1969 book [_Perceptrons_](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Perceptrons_(book) "Perceptrons (book)").[\[109\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMinskyPapert1969-120) It suggested that there were severe limitations to what perceptrons could do and that Rosenblatt's predictions had been grossly exaggerated. The effect of the book was that virtually no research was funded in [connectionism](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Connectionism "Connectionism") for 10 years.[\[110\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202122-121) The competition for government funding ended with the victory of symbolic AI approaches over neural networks.[\[107\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENielson2005-117)[\[108\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlazaran_Rodriguez1991-118)

Minsky (who had worked on [SNARC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stochastic_Neural_Analog_Reinforcement_Calculator "Stochastic Neural Analog Reinforcement Calculator")) became a staunch objector to pure connectionist AI. [Widrow](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Bernard_Widrow "Bernard Widrow") (who had worked on [ADALINE](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ADALINE "ADALINE")) turned to adaptive signal processing. The [SRI](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/SRI_International "SRI International") group (which worked on MINOS) turned to symbolic AI and robotics.[\[107\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENielson2005-117)[\[108\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEOlazaran_Rodriguez1991-118)

The main problem was the inability to train multilayered networks (versions of [backpropagation](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Backpropagation "Backpropagation") had already been used in other fields, but it was unknown to these researchers).[\[111\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTESchmidhuber2022-122)[\[110\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202122-121) The AI community became aware of backpropagation in the 1980s,[\[112\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202124-123) and, in the 21st century, neural networks would become enormously successful, fulfilling all of Rosenblatt's optimistic predictions. Rosenblatt did not live to see this, however, as he died in a boating accident in 1971.[\[113\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993105-124)

### Optimism

The first generation of AI researchers made the following predictions about their work:

- 1958, [H. A. Simon](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/H._A._Simon "H. A. Simon") and [Allen Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell"): "within ten years a digital computer will be the world's chess champion" and "within ten years a digital computer will discover and prove an important new mathematical theorem."[\[114\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-125)
- 1965, H. A. Simon: "machines will be capable, within twenty years, of doing any work a man can do."[\[115\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-126)
- 1967, [Marvin Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky"): "Within a generation... the problem of creating 'artificial intelligence' will substantially be solved."[\[116\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-127)
- 1970, Marvin Minsky (in [_Life_ magazine](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Life_magazine "Life magazine")): "In from three to eight years we will have a machine with the general intelligence of an average human being."[\[117\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDarrach1970-128)[\[l\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-131)

### Financing

In June 1963, [MIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIT "MIT") received a $2.2 million grant from the newly formed Advanced Research Projects Agency (ARPA, later known as [DARPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA")). The money was used to fund [project MAC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Project_MAC "Project MAC") which subsumed the "AI Group" founded by [Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky") and [McCarthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)") five years earlier. DARPA continued to provide $3 million each year until the 1970s.[\[120\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199364%E2%80%9365-132) DARPA made similar grants to [Newell](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Allen_Newell "Allen Newell") and [Simon's](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Herbert_A._Simon "Herbert A. Simon") program at [Carnegie Mellon University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Carnegie_Mellon_University "Carnegie Mellon University") and to [Stanford University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_University "Stanford University")'s [AI Lab](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stanford_AI_Lab "Stanford AI Lab"), founded by [John McCarthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)") in 1963.[\[121\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199394-133) Another important AI laboratory was established at [Edinburgh University](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Edinburgh_University "Edinburgh University") by [Donald Michie](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Donald_Michie "Donald Michie") in 1965.[\[122\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHowe1994-134) These four institutions would continue to be the main centers of AI research and funding in academia for many years.[\[123\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199351-135)[\[m\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-137)

The money was given with few strings attached: [J. C. R. Licklider](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._C._R._Licklider "J. C. R. Licklider"), then the director of ARPA, believed that his organization should "fund people, not projects!" and allowed researchers to pursue whatever directions might interest them.[\[125\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier199365-138) This created a freewheeling atmosphere at MIT that gave birth to the [hacker culture](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hacker_culture "Hacker culture"),[\[126\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-139) but this "hands off" approach did not last.

## First AI winter (1974–1980)

In the 1970s, AI was subject to critiques and financial setbacks. AI researchers had failed to appreciate the difficulty of the problems they faced. Their tremendous optimism had raised public expectations impossibly high, and when the promised results failed to materialize, funding targeted at AI was severely reduced.[\[127\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993163%E2%80%93196-140) The lack of success indicated that the techniques being used by AI researchers at the time were insufficient to achieve their goals.[\[128\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEDreyfus1972-141)[\[129\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTELighthill1973-142)

These setbacks did not affect the growth and progress of the field, however. The funding cuts only impacted a handful of major laboratories[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh2023-143) and the critiques were largely ignored.[\[131\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993143-144) General public interest in the field continued to grow,[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh2023-143) the number of researchers increased dramatically,[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh2023-143) and new ideas were explored in [logic programming](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Logic_programming "Logic programming"), [commonsense reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_reasoning "Commonsense reasoning") and many other areas. Historian Thomas Haigh argued in 2023 that there was no winter,[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh2023-143) and AI researcher [Nils Nilsson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Nils_John_Nilsson "Nils John Nilsson") described this period as the most "exciting" time to work in AI.[\[132\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENilsson20091-145)

### Problems

In the early seventies, the capabilities of AI programs were limited. Even the most impressive could only handle trivial versions of the problems they were supposed to solve;[\[n\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-147) all the programs were, in some sense, "toys".[\[134\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993146-148) AI researchers had begun to run into several limits that would be only conquered decades later, and others that still stymie the field in the 2020s:

- **[Limited computer power](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moore%27s_law "Moore's law")**: There was not enough memory or processing speed to accomplish anything truly useful.[\[o\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-150) For example: Ross Quillian's successful work on natural language was demonstrated with a vocabulary of only 20 words, because that was all that would fit in memory.[\[136\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993146%E2%80%93148-151) [Hans Moravec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hans_Moravec "Hans Moravec") argued in 1976 that computers were still millions of times too weak to exhibit intelligence. He suggested an analogy: artificial intelligence requires computer power in the same way that aircraft require [horsepower](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Horsepower "Horsepower"). Below a certain threshold, it's impossible, but, as power [increases](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moore%27s_law "Moore's law"), eventually it could become easy. "With enough horsepower," he wrote, "anything will fly".[\[137\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoravec1976-152)[\[p\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-154)
- **[Intractability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intractability_(complexity) "Intractability (complexity)") and the [combinatorial explosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Combinatorial_explosion "Combinatorial explosion")**: In 1972 [Richard Karp](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Richard_Karp "Richard Karp") (building on [Stephen Cook](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Stephen_Cook "Stephen Cook")'s 1971 [theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Cook%27s_theorem "Cook's theorem")) showed there are [many problems](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Karp%27s_21_NP-complete_problems "Karp's 21 NP-complete problems") that can only be solved in [exponential time](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Exponential_time "Exponential time"). Finding optimal solutions to these problems requires extraordinary amounts of computer time, except when the problems are trivial. This limitation applied to all symbolic AI programs that used search trees and meant that many of the "toy" solutions used by AI would never scale to useful systems.[\[133\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202121-146)[\[129\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTELighthill1973-142)
- **[Moravec's paradox](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Moravec%27s_paradox "Moravec's paradox")**: Early AI research had been very successful at getting computers to do "intelligent" tasks like proving theorems, solving geometry problems and playing chess. Their success at these intelligent tasks convinced them that the problem of intelligent behavior had been largely solved.[\[139\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004456-155)[\[140\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks2002-156) However, they utterly failed to make progress on "unintelligent" tasks like recognizing a face or crossing a room without bumping into anything.[\[139\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck2004456-155)[\[141\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMoravec198815%E2%80%9316-157) By the 1980s, researchers would realize that symbolic reasoning was utterly unsuited for these perceptual and sensorimotor tasks and that there were limits to this approach.[\[140\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEBrooks2002-156)
- **The breadth of [commonsense knowledge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_knowledge "Commonsense knowledge")**: Many important artificial intelligence applications like [vision](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_vision "Computer vision") or [natural language](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Natural_language "Natural language") require enormous amounts of information about the world: the program needs to have some idea of what it might be looking at or what it is talking about. This requires that the program know most of the same things about the world that a child does. Researchers soon discovered that this was a vast amount of information with billions of atomic facts. No one in 1970 could build a database large enough and no one knew how a program might learn so much information.[\[142\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-158)
- **Representing [commonsense reasoning](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_reasoning "Commonsense reasoning")**: Several related problems[\[q\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-159) appeared when researchers tried to represent commonsense reasoning using formal logic or symbols. Descriptions of very ordinary deductions tended to get longer and longer the more one worked on them, as more and more exceptions, clarifications and distinctions were required. [\[r\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-160) However, when people thought about ordinary concepts, they did not rely on precise definitions, rather they seemed to make hundreds of imprecise assumptions, correcting them when necessary using their entire body of commonsense knowledge. [Gerald Sussman](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Gerald_Sussman "Gerald Sussman") observed that "using precise language to describe essentially imprecise concepts doesn't make them any more precise."[\[143\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-161)

### Decrease in funding

See also: [AI winter](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/AI_winter "AI winter")

The agencies that funded AI research, such as the [British government](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/British_government "British government"), [DARPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA") and the [National Research Council](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council "United States National Research Council") (NRC) became frustrated with the lack of progress and eventually cut off almost all funding for undirected AI research. The pattern began in 1966 when the [Automatic Language Processing Advisory Committee](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Automatic_Language_Processing_Advisory_Committee "Automatic Language Processing Advisory Committee") (ALPAC) report criticized machine translation efforts. After spending $20 million, the [NRC](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/United_States_National_Research_Council "United States National Research Council") ended all support.[\[144\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-162) In 1973, the [Lighthill report](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Lighthill_report "Lighthill report") on the state of AI research in the UK criticized the failure of AI to achieve its "grandiose objectives" and led to the dismantling of AI research in that country.[\[145\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-163) (The report specifically mentioned the [combinatorial explosion](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Combinatorial_explosion "Combinatorial explosion") problem as a reason for AI's failings.)[\[129\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTELighthill1973-142)[\[133\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202121-146)[\[s\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-165) DARPA was deeply disappointed with researchers working on the [Speech Understanding Research](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Speech_recognition "Speech recognition") program at CMU and canceled an annual grant of $3 million.[\[147\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993115%E2%80%93116-166)[\[t\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-167)

[Hans Moravec](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hans_Moravec "Hans Moravec") blamed the crisis on the unrealistic predictions of his colleagues. "Many researchers were caught up in a web of increasing exaggeration."[\[148\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTECrevier1993115-168)[\[u\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-169) However, there was another issue: since the passage of the [Mansfield Amendment](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Mansfield_Amendment "Mansfield Amendment") in 1969, [DARPA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/DARPA "DARPA") had been under increasing pressure to fund "mission-oriented direct research, rather than basic undirected research". Funding for the creative, freewheeling exploration that had gone on in the 60s would not come from DARPA, which instead directed money at specific projects with clear objectives, such as [autonomous](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Autonomous "Autonomous") tanks and [battle management](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Battle_management "Battle management") systems.[\[149\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENRC1999under_%22Shift_to_Applied_Research_Increases_Investment.%22-170)[\[v\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-172)

The major laboratories (MIT, Stanford, CMU and Edinburgh) had been receiving generous support from their governments, and when it was withdrawn, these were the only places that were seriously impacted by the budget cuts. The thousands of researchers outside these institutions and the many thousands that were joining the field were unaffected.[\[130\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEHaigh2023-143)

### Philosophical and ethical critiques

See also: [Philosophy of artificial intelligence](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Philosophy_of_artificial_intelligence "Philosophy of artificial intelligence")

Several philosophers had strong objections to the claims being made by AI researchers. One of the earliest was [John Lucas](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Lucas_(philosopher) "John Lucas (philosopher)"), who argued that [Gödel's incompleteness theorem](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/G%C3%B6del%27s_incompleteness_theorem "Gödel's incompleteness theorem") showed that a [formal system](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Formal_system "Formal system") (such as a computer program) could never see the truth of certain statements, while a human being could.[\[151\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-173) [Hubert Dreyfus](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Hubert_Dreyfus "Hubert Dreyfus") ridiculed the broken promises of the 1960s and critiqued the assumptions of AI, arguing that human reasoning actually involved very little "symbol processing" and a great deal of [embodied](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Embodied_cognition "Embodied cognition"), [instinctive](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Instinct "Instinct"), unconscious " [know how](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ready-to-hand "Ready-to-hand")".[\[w\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-175)[\[153\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-176) [John Searle](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_Searle "John Searle")'s [Chinese Room](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Chinese_Room "Chinese Room") argument, presented in 1980, attempted to show that a program could not be said to "understand" the symbols that it uses (a quality called " [intentionality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intentionality "Intentionality")"). If the symbols have no meaning for the machine, Searle argued, then the machine can not be described as "thinking".[\[154\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-177)

These critiques were not taken seriously by AI researchers. Problems like [intractability](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intractability_(complexity) "Intractability (complexity)") and [commonsense knowledge](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Commonsense_reasoning "Commonsense reasoning") seemed much more immediate and serious. It was unclear what difference " [know how](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Ready-to-hand "Ready-to-hand")" or " [intentionality](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Intentionality "Intentionality")" made to an actual computer program. MIT's [Minsky](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Marvin_Minsky "Marvin Minsky") said of Dreyfus and Searle, "they misunderstand, and should be ignored."[\[155\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-178) Dreyfus, who also taught at [MIT](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/MIT "MIT"), was given a cold shoulder: he later said that AI researchers "dared not be seen having lunch with me."[\[156\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-179) [Joseph Weizenbaum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Weizenbaum "Joseph Weizenbaum"), the author of [ELIZA](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/ELIZA "ELIZA"), was also an outspoken critic of Dreyfus' positions, but he "deliberately made it plain that \[his AI colleagues' treatment of Dreyfus\] was not the way to treat a human being,"[\[x\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-181) and was unprofessional and childish.[\[158\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTENewquist1994276-182)

Weizenbaum began to have serious ethical doubts about AI when [Kenneth Colby](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Kenneth_Colby "Kenneth Colby") wrote a "computer program which can conduct [psychotherapeutic](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Psychotherapeutic "Psychotherapeutic") dialogue" based on ELIZA.[\[159\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEColbyWattGilbert1966148-183)[\[160\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEWeizenbaum19765,_6-184)[\[y\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-186) Weizenbaum was disturbed that Colby saw a mindless program as a serious therapeutic tool. A feud began, and the situation was not helped when Colby did not credit Weizenbaum for his contribution to the program. In 1976, [Weizenbaum](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Joseph_Weizenbaum "Joseph Weizenbaum") published _[Computer Power and Human Reason](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Computer_Power_and_Human_Reason "Computer Power and Human Reason")_ which argued that the misuse of artificial intelligence has the potential to devalue human life.[\[162\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-187)

### Logic at Stanford, CMU, and Edinburgh

Logic was introduced into AI research as early as 1958, by [John McCarthy](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/John_McCarthy_(computer_scientist) "John McCarthy (computer scientist)") in his [Advice Taker](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/Advice_Taker "Advice Taker") proposal.[\[163\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTEMcCorduck200451-188)[\[88\]](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/History_of_artificial_intelligence#cite_note-FOOTNOTERussellNorvig202119-97) In 1963, [J. Alan Robinson](https://en.wikipedia.org/wiki/J._Alan_Robinson "J.
